The First Proof: Angels and Intellectual Pleasures
Ghazali presents the first proof for the superiority of intellectual pleasures using the example of angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first proof for the superiority of intellectual pleasures over bodily ones, based on the example of angels.

Core Idea
Angels lack bodily pleasures yet are nobler than beasts—proving intellectual pleasures are superior.

Ghazali begins his argument by comparing two kinds of beings: angels and animals. He observes that angels, despite having no bodily pleasures of eating or sex, are far nobler than beasts. Their pleasure comes from knowledge and awareness of God. This sets up the first proof that intellectual pleasures are more worthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali introduces two proofs but only gives the first here.
2
Analyze the angelic comparison
Contrast the state of angels with that of beasts and swine. Identify what pleasures angels lack and what kind of pleasure they have instead.
Angels have pleasure of ‘awareness of their perfection’ and ‘nearness to God in attributes’.
3
Understand the metaphysical basis
Ghazali adds that existence is ordered hierarchically through intermediaries. Closeness to the intermediaries means a higher rank. This supports the conclusion that intellectual pleasures are linked to higher existence.
Nearness here is not physical but in attributes and rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن اللذات العقلية ، أشرف من اللذات الجسمانية ، أمران : أحدهما : أن حال الملائكة أشرف من حال السباع والخنازير من البهائم وليست لها اللذات الجسمية ، من الجمال والأكل ، وإنما لها لذة الشعور بكمالها وجمالها ، الذي خصت به فى نفسها ، فى اطلاعها على حقائق الأشياء ، وقربها من رب العالمين فى الصفات ، لا فى المكان ورتبة الوجود ، فإن الموجودات ، الله تعالى ، على ترتيب ، و بوسائط ، فالذى يقرب من الوسائط ، حصلت له رتبته لا محالة ، أعلى مما دونها . والثانى ، أن الإنسان أيضاً ، قد يؤثر اللذات العقلية ، على الجسمية ، فإن من يتمكن من غلبة عدوه والشماتة به ، يهجر في تحصيلها ، ملاذ الأنكحة والأطعمة ، بل قد يهجر الأكل طول النهار ، فى لذة غلبة الشطرنج والنرد ، مع خسة الأمر فيهما ، ولا يحس بألم الجوع ، وكذلك المتشوف إلى الحشمة ، وإلى الرئاسة ، يتردد ، بين انخرام حشمته ، وبين قضاء الوطر من عشيقته ، مثلا ، بحيث يعرفه غيره، وينتشر عنه ، فيؤثر الحشمة ، ويترك قضاء الوطر ، ويستحقر ذلك ، محافظة على ماء الوجه ، فيكون ذلك لا محالة ألذ عنده . بل ربما يهجم الشجاع ، على جم غفير من الشجعان ، مستحقراً خطر الموت ، شغفاً بما يتوهمه بعد الموت ، من لذة الثناء والإطراء عليه فإذن اللذات العقلية الأخروية ، أفضل من اللذات الجسمية الدنيوية ، ولولا ذلك لما قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه سلم : يقول الله تعالى : [ أعددت لعبادى الصالحين ، ما لا ، ما لا عين رأت ، ولا أذن سمعت ، ولا خطر على قلب بشر ] . وقال تعالى : [ فلا تعلم نفس ما أخفي لهم من قرة أعين ] . فهذا وجه الحاجة إلى العلم . والنافع من جملته العلوم العقلية المحضة ، هى العلم بالله تعالى ، وصفاته ، وملائكته ، وكتبه ، وكيفية وجود الأشياء منه ، وما وراء ذلك ، إن كان وسيلة إليه ، فهو نافع لأجله ، وإن لم يكن وسيلة إليه ، كالنحو واللغة والشعر
And the proof that intellectual pleasures are more noble than bodily pleasures consists of two points: First: that the state of angels is more noble than the state of beasts and swine among animals, and they do not have bodily pleasures of beauty and eating; rather, they have the pleasure of sensing their perfection and beauty with which they are uniquely characterized in themselves, in their awareness of the realities of things and their nearness to the Lord of the worlds in attributes, not in place and rank of existence. For existents from God Most High are in an order and through intermediaries, so whoever is near to the intermediaries necessarily has a rank higher than those below. Second: that man also sometimes prefers intellectual pleasures over bodily ones. For one who is able to defeat his enemy and gloat over him abandons in attainment of that the delights of sex and food; indeed, he may abandon food all day long in the pleasure of winning at chess and backgammon, despite the baseness of the matter, and does not feel the pain of hunger. Similarly, one who aspires to dignity and leadership hesitates between the loss of his dignity and the fulfillment of his desire from his beloved, for example, when others would know of it and it would become public, so he prefers dignity, abandons fulfillment of desire, and scorns it in order to preserve his honor, and that inevitably is more pleasurable to him. Indeed, perhaps the brave man attacks a great multitude of warriors, scorning the danger of death, out of passion for what he imagines after death of the pleasure of praise and acclaim. So then the intellectual pleasures of the afterlife are better than the bodily pleasures of this world. Were that not so, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) would not have said: 'Allah Most High says: I have prepared for My righteous servants what no eye has seen, no ear has heard, nor has it occurred to any human heart.' And He Most High said: 'No soul knows what joy is kept hidden for them.' This is the aspect of the need for knowledge. And the beneficial among the purely intellectual sciences is knowledge of God Most High, His attributes, His angels, His books, the manner of existence of things from Him, and whatever is beyond that—if it is a means to it, it is beneficial for its sake; if it is not a means to it, like grammar, language, and poetry.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the superiority of intellectual pleasures over bodily pleasures, followed by a conclusion about the need for knowledge. Ghazali begins by stating that the proof consists of two points. The first proof is based on the status of angels: angels are nobler than beasts and swine, yet they have no bodily pleasures; rather, their pleasure lies in awareness of their own perfection and beauty, and in their knowledge of the realities of things and their nearness to God in attributes (not in place). This shows that the highest pleasure is intellectual. The second proof is from human experience: people often prefer intellectual or emotional pleasures over bodily ones. Examples include a person who abandons food and sex to gloat over an enemy, a chess player who forgets hunger, and one who sacrifices desire for dignity and honor. Even the brave who risk death for posthumous praise show that intellectual pleasures (like honor) are preferred. From these proofs, Ghazali concludes that the intellectual pleasures of the afterlife are superior to bodily pleasures of this world. He supports this with a divine hadith and a Quranic verse indicating unimaginable joys. Finally, he states that this is the reason for the need for knowledge; the beneficial purely intellectual sciences are knowledge of God, His attributes, angels, books, and how things exist from Him, and other sciences are beneficial only as means to these.

Key Terms
اللذات العقلية
اللذات الجسمانية
الملائكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels have no pleasure at all because they lack bodily sensations.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that angels have intellectual pleasure from awareness and nearness to God, which is superior.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the nature of angelic pleasure according to Ghazali?
A) They enjoy food and beauty.
B) They derive pleasure from their awareness of their perfection and knowledge of realities.
C) They have no pleasure at all.
D) They only experience pleasure in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that intellectual pleasures are superior because even the noblest beings (angels) lack bodily pleasures yet have a higher form of pleasure through knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between indulging a bodily desire and spending time seeking knowledge of Allah, which do you instinctively prefer? What does that show about your current rank in the hierarchy of existence?
?
Angels feel pleasure in their nearness to God. Do you feel genuine pleasure in your acts of worship and learning? If not, what is missing in your heart?
?
If all bodily pleasures were taken from you—health, wealth, family—would you still have any pleasure left? Would you be like an angel or like a beast?

The Second Proof: Human Experience and the Need for Knowledge
Ghazali presents the second proof from human experience and concludes with the need for knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second proof from human experience and his conclusion that the pursuit of knowledge of God is the highest goal.

Core Idea
Human choices—preferring honor, victory, or praise over bodily pleasures—prove the superiority of intellectual pleasures.

Having given the angelic proof, Ghazali now turns to human behavior. He draws on everyday examples: a person gloating over an enemy, a chess player forgetting hunger, a leader sacrificing desire for honor, and a brave warrior risking death for fame. These all show that intellectual pleasures are often preferred. He then concludes that the highest knowledge is knowledge of God and the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic passage from 'الثاني' to the end. Pay attention to the examples.
This passage is longer; read carefully.
2
Identify the examples
Ghazali gives four examples: gloating over enemy, chess/backgammon, honor vs desire, bravery for praise. Write them down.
Notice that all involve sacrificing bodily pleasure for a non-bodily goal.
3
Connect to the conclusion
How do these examples lead to the statement that intellectual pleasures of the afterlife are better?
Ghazali uses them to show that intellectual pleasures are naturally preferred even in worldly matters.
4
Understand the need for knowledge
Read the final part about the beneficial sciences. What is the ultimate purpose of knowledge?
Only knowledge of God and related sciences is beneficial; other sciences are only means.

The Rational Soul Becomes an Intellectual World
Ghazali defines the rational soul's specific perfection as becoming an intellectual world mirroring all existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's concept of the rational soul's perfection as becoming an intellectual world that mirrors the entire cosmos.

Core Idea
The rational soul's highest perfection is to become a mirror of all existence.

Having established the principles, Ghazali now turns to the purpose: to describe the specific perfection of the rational soul. This perfection is not merely theoretical but involves a transformative process where the soul becomes an intellectual world, imprinted with the order of all things, from the First Principle to the material cosmos. Let us explore this profound vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, noticing the sequence from the First Principle to the celestial bodies.
2
Identify the stages
Notice the progressive enumeration: beginning from the principle of the whole, proceeding to absolute spirituality, then spirituality attached to bodies, then celestial bodies, until the form of all existence is completed.
3
Grasp the final state
Understand that the soul becomes an intelligible world parallel to the existent world, witnessing absolute beauty and united with it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this view positions intellectual perfection as the highest human goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا تقررت هذه الأصول ، فيجب أن ننصرف إلى الغرض الذي نؤمه ، فنقول : إن النفس الناطقة ، كمالها الخاص بها ، أن تصير عالماً عقلياً ، مرتسما فيه صورة الكل ، والنظام المعقول فى الكل ، والخير الفائض فى الكل ، مبتدئاً من مبدأ الكل ، سالكاً إلى الجواهر الشريفة ، فالروحانية المطلقة ، ثم الروحانية المتعلقة نوعاً ما ، من التعلق بالأبدان ، ثم الأجسام العلوية بهيئاتها وقواها ، ثم تستمر كذلك ، حتى تستوفى فى نفسها هيئة الوجود كله ؛ فتنقلب عالماً معقولا ، موازياً للعالم الموجود كله ، مشاهداً لما هو الحسن المطلق ، والخير المطلق ، والجمال الحق ، ومتحداً به ، ومنتقشاً بمثاله وهيأته ، ومنخرطاً في سلكه ، وصائراً من جوهره . وإذا قيس هذا بالكمالات المعشوقة ، التى للقوى الأخرى ، وجد في المرتبة ، التي بحيث يقبح تهافت الفلاسفة . وقوله : [ أعددت لعبادي الصالحين، ما لا عين رأت ، ولا أذن سمعت ولا خطر على قلب بشر ] فكذلك وجود هذه الأمور الشريفة ، لا يدل على نفى غيرها ، بل الجمع بين الأمرين أكمل ، والموعود به أكمل الأمور ، وهو ممكن ، فيجب التصديق به على وفق الشرع
When these principles are established, we must turn to the purpose we intend, and say: indeed the rational soul, its specific perfection is that it becomes an intellectual world, in which is imprinted the image of the whole, the intelligible order in the whole, and the good flowing in the whole, beginning from the principle of the whole, proceeding to the noble essences: the absolute spirituality, then the spirituality attached in some way to bodies, then the celestial bodies with their forms and powers, then it continues thus until it completes in itself the form of all existence; so it is transformed into an intelligible world, parallel to the entire existent world, witnessing what is absolute beauty, absolute good, true beauty, and becoming united with it, and imprinted with its likeness and form, and incorporated into its order, and becoming of its essence. And if this is measured against the beloved perfections of the other powers, it is found to be in a rank such that the philosophers' incoherence becomes ugly. And His saying: [I have prepared for My righteous servants what no eye has seen, nor ear heard, nor has it occurred to the heart of man] – so likewise, the existence of these noble matters does not indicate the negation of others; rather, combining both matters is more perfect, and what is promised is the most perfect of matters, and it is possible, so it must be affirmed in accordance with the law.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is the central thesis of the section. After laying down three principles (أصول), Ghazali announces the purpose: to describe the rational soul's perfection (كمالها الخاص). The soul's perfection is to become an intellectual world (عالماً عقلياً) that mirrors the entire existing world. It begins from the First Principle (God), then encompasses the spiritual substances (absolute and attached to bodies), then the celestial bodies, and continues until it has the form of all existence. At that point, the soul becomes an intelligible world parallel to the material world, witnessing absolute beauty and goodness, and united with it. Ghazali then cautions that this intellectual perfection does not negate the sensual pleasures promised in the afterlife (referencing the hadith). Rather, combining both is more perfect, and since it is possible, we must believe in the revealed promises. The mention of 'the philosophers' incoherence' (تهافت الفلاسفة) alludes to his own critique of the philosophers who deny bodily resurrection.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
عالم عقلي
مبدأ الكل
الجواهر الشريفة
الحسن المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual perfection is merely acquiring knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali describes it as a transformative becoming, an actual imprinting of the entire order of existence in the soul.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the specific perfection of the rational soul?
A) Acquiring theoretical knowledge
B) Becoming an intellectual world mirroring all existence
C) Achieving moral virtue
D) Attaining pleasure

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's perfection is to become an intellectual world that mirrors the entire cosmos, from the First Principle to the material bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul's perfection is to become an intellectual world mirroring all existence, what does it say about your current priorities that you spend more time on material pursuits than on this transformation? Are you willing to reorder your day to begin from the First Principle?
?
Ghazali says the soul must start from the First Principle and proceed through the hierarchy. Where in your life do you start from something other than God—your career, relationships, or comfort? What would it cost you to truly make God your first principle?
?
You are called to be imprinted with the likeness of absolute beauty and good. Do you honestly believe that your soul is capable of such union, or have you settled for a lesser goal? What step will you take today to begin this ascent?

Intellectual Perfection Does Not Negate Sensory Afterlife
Ghazali argues that intellectual perfection does not negate sensual rewards; both are combined for the most perfect state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Ghazali, intellectual perfection does not contradict the sensory pleasures promised in paradise; rather, both are combined for the most perfect state.

Core Idea
The highest perfection combines intellectual vision and sensory pleasures.

After describing the intellectual perfection of the soul, Ghazali anticipates a possible objection: does this intellectual vision make bodily pleasures obsolete? He replies by referencing the prophetic hadith about rewards no eye has seen, and argues that the existence of intellectual perfection does not negate sensory pleasures; rather, the most perfect state includes both. This lesson explores that reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Ghazali measures the intellectual rank and then cites the hadith.
2
Identify the key claim
Ghazali says the existence of these noble matters does not indicate negation of others. Understand that 'these noble matters' refers to the intellectual perfections described earlier.
3
Understand the principle
He states that combining both (intellectual and sensory) is more perfect, and since it is possible, we must believe in the revealed promises.
4
Reflect on the reason
Why does Ghazali emphasize possibility? Because if something is possible, and revelation affirms it, we must accept it even if it seems beyond our experience.

Intellectual Perfection as the Path to True Happiness
Ghazali describes the intellectual knowledge constituting true happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali describes the intellectual knowledge of metaphysical principles and divine attributes that leads to true happiness.

Core Idea
True happiness is achieved through intellectual vision of the divine order and attachment to the First Principle.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali outlines the kind of intellectual perfection that he associates with true happiness—not mere worldly success but a deep comprehension of the separate principles, the final causes, and the order emanating from the First Principle. He also emphasizes the role of demonstrated certainty (burhan) and the need to conceive divine unity without multiplicity or change. This description sets up the philosophical ideal that he will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Ghazali's description come through before breaking it down.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the components of intellectual perfection
List the elements Ghazali includes: real conception of separate principles, certain affirmation via demonstration, knowledge of final causes of universal motions, comprehension of the whole order, understanding of divine care, and reflection on the First Principle's unity and knowledge.
Notice that he excludes infinite particulars.
3
Connect insight to happiness
Ghazali states that the more insight (istibsar) one gains, the more preparation for happiness increases. Reflect on why knowledge of the metaphysical structure is seen as preparatory for happiness.
4
Consider the contrast between worlds
Notice the final sentence: disengagement from this world occurs through strengthening the relationship with the higher world. This shows the practical outcome of intellectual perfection.
This longing prevents turning to worldly attachments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأظن أن ذلك ، أن يتصور الإنسان المبادئ المفارقة ، تصوراً حقيقياً ، ويصدق بها تصديقاً يقينياً ، لوجودها عنده بالبرهان ، ويعرف العلل الغائية ، للأمور الواقعة في الحركات الكلية ، دون الجزئية التي لا تتناهى .
ويتقرر عنده هيأة الكل ، ونسب أجزاء بعضها إلى بعض ، والنظام الآخذ من المبدأ الأول ، إلى أقصى الموجودات الواقعة في ترتيبه ويتصور العناية وكيفيتها ، ويتحقق أن الذات المتقدمة للكل ، أي وجود يخصها ؟ ! وأية وحدة تخصها ؟ ! وأنها كيف تعرف ، حتى لا يلحقها تكثر ولا تغير ، بوجه من الوجوه ؟ !
وكيف ترتبت نسبة الموجودات إليها ؟ !
ثم كلما ازداد الناظر استبصاراً ، ازداد للسعادة استعداداً
وكأنه ليس يتبرأ الإنسان عن هذا العالم وعلائقه ، إلا أن يكون أكد العلاقة مع ذلك العالم ، فصار له شوق إلى ما هناك ، وعشق ما هناك ، يصده عن الالتفات إلى ما خلفه جملة
And I think that that is: that man conceive the separate principles in a real conception, and affirm them with certain affirmation, because of their existence according to him through demonstration, and know the final causes of the things occurring in universal motions, not of the particular ones that are infinite. And the form of the whole and the relations of its parts to each other and the order taking from the First Principle to the farthest existents falling in its arrangement become established for him; and he conceive [divine] care and its modality, and ascertain the Self prior to the whole: what existence is proper to it? and what unity is proper to it? and how it knows, so that no multiplicity or change attaches to it in any way? and how the relation of existents is ordered to it? Then the more the observer gains insight, the more his preparation for happiness increases. And it is as if man does not disengage from this world and its attachments except by strengthening the relationship with that world; so there comes to be for him a longing for what is there and a love for what is there, which prevents him from turning his attention to what is behind him altogether.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage continues the answer by describing the intellectual perfection that constitutes true happiness. Ghazali says that happiness involves the real perception (تصور حقيقي) of the separate principles (المبادئ المفارقة), which are the immaterial realities, and affirming them with certainty through demonstration (برهان). It also requires knowledge of the final causes (العلل الغائية) of universal motions, not of infinite particulars. Furthermore, one must comprehend the structure of the whole (هيأة الكل), the relations of its parts, the order emanating from the First Principle (المبدأ الأول) to the farthest existents, and the nature of divine care (العناية). One must also understand the Self that is prior to all (الذات المتقدمة للكل), its existence, its unique unity, and how it knows without multiplicity or change. The more one gains insight, the more one is prepared for happiness. This love for the higher world turns the soul away from this world completely. This description aligns with philosophical theology.

Key Terms

برهان
علل غائية
عناية
ذات متقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali fully endorses the philosophical description of happiness as purely intellectual.
Correction: Ghazali presents this description but contextually he adds a moral and religious component, as part of his critique of the philosophers.

Ghazali's Three Divisions: Rational Alternatives to Eternal World
Ghazali presents three rational alternatives to the philosophers' necessary eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's three-phase schema of divine acts, which demonstrates that an eternal will does not necessitate an eternal world.

Core Idea
An eternal will does not force an eternal world; God could have created in stages.

Ghazali has shown that the philosophers' reasoning about an eternal will necessarily implying an eternal world is not conclusive. He now presents a thought experiment: three phases of divine action that are rationally possible, though some are restricted by revelation. This passage lays out that alternative schema.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the three divisions. Notice how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' implication first.
2
Identify the four phases
List the phases: God alone, creation of world in observed order, second order in Paradise, annihilation of all but God. Note the fourth is restricted by revelation.
3
Understand the rational possibility
Ghazali argues that these phases are not rationally impossible. Reflect on why he uses this to counter the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب، أن هذا استمداد مسألة قدم العالم، وأن المشيئة قديمة، من فليكن العالم قديماً، وقد أبطلنا ذلك وبينا أنه لا يبعد في العقل، وضع ثلاثة أقسام، وهى: أن يكون الله تعالى موجوداً، ولا عالم. ثم يخلق العالم، على النظام المشاهد ثم يستأنف نظاماً ثانياً، وهو الموعود في الجنة. ثم يعدم الكل، حتى لا يبقى إلا الله تعالى، وهو ممكن، لولا أن الشرع قد ورد، بأن الثواب والعقاب، والجنة والنار، لا آخر لها وهذه المسألة كيفما دارت تنبنى على مسألتين: إحداهما: حدوث العالم، وجواز حصول حادث من قديم. والثانية: خرق العادات، بخلق المسببات، دون الأسباب، أو إحداث أسباب، على منهج آخر غير معتاد، وقد فرغنا من المسألتين جميعاً، والله أعلم.
And the answer: that this is deriving from the issue of the eternity of the world, and that the will is eternal, so let the world be eternal. And we have refuted that and shown that it is not rationally remote to posit three divisions, which are: that Allah exists and no world (exists). Then He creates the world in the observed order. Then He initiates a second order, which is promised in Paradise. Then He annihilates everything, such that nothing remains except Allah. And that is possible, were it not that the divine law has come that reward and punishment, and Paradise and Hell have no end. And this issue, however it is considered, is based on two issues: one of them: the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) and the permissibility of an originated thing occurring from an eternal being. And the second: the breaking of habits (kharq al-ʿādāt) by creating effects without their causes, or creating causes on a different pattern than the usual. And we have finished both issues. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is an answer and conclusion to the previous argument. Ghazali first states that the philosophers' reasoning (that the eternal will implies a regular repetitive order) is merely a borrowing from the issue of the world's eternity (qidam al-ʿālam). They assume the will is eternal and therefore the world must be eternal. But Ghazali claims he has already refuted that assumption and shown that it is not rationally impossible (lā yubʿud fī al-ʿaql) to posit instead three divisions (aqsām) of the divine acts: 1) God exists alone without any world; 2) then He creates the world in the observed order; 3) then He initiates a second order, that is the promised order in Paradise; 4) then He annihilates everything so that only God remains. This fourth step is possible except that the revealed law (sharʿ) states that reward, punishment, Paradise, and Hell are everlasting, so annihilation does not apply to them. Ghazali then notes that the whole issue (whether the world can have a beginning or must be eternal) depends on two more fundamental issues: (a) the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) and the possibility of an originated thing coming from an eternal agent; (b) the breaking of natural habits (kharq al-ʿādāt) – i.e., whether God can create effects without their usual causes or create new causes on an unconventional pattern. Ghazali declares that he has already fully treated both issues earlier in the book. This passage thus serves as a summary and transition, affirming the possibility of a non-eternal world and divine intervention, against the philosophers' necessitarian view.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
النظام المشاهد
النظام الموعود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three divisions are Ghazali's final doctrine about the actual sequence of events.
Correction: Ghazali presents them as rationally possible alternatives, not as his definitive creed. He acknowledges that revelation restricts the annihilation phase.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which phase is restricted by divine law (sharʿ)?
A) God exists alone without world
B) Creation of the world in observed order
C) Initiation of the second order in Paradise
D) Annihilation of everything except God

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that an eternal will does not necessitate an eternal world; multiple sequences of creation, including a future order and final annihilation, are rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could have created the world at any moment, why did He choose the moment He did? How does this affect your trust in His timing for your own life?
?
The passage shows that God's will is not bound by human logic of necessity. In what area of your life are you demanding that God act according to your understanding?
?
Everything except God will be annihilated. What are you holding onto that you need to release so that your heart is attached only to the Eternal?

Two Foundational Issues: World's Origin and Breaking Habits
Ghazali encapsulates the entire debate into two fundamental issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational issues (ḥudūth al-ʿālam and kharq al-ʿādāt) on which the entire debate about the world's eternity rests.

Core Idea
Two issues underlie all discussion: whether the world began, and whether God can break natural habits.

After presenting his counter-schema, Ghazali identifies the two core issues that the whole debate depends on. These are the temporal origination of the world and the breaking of natural habits. Ghazali claims he has already resolved both. This lesson clarifies what these issues mean and why they are central.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the phrase 'تنبنى على مسألتين'. Understand that these are the pillars of the argument.
2
Understand the first issue: Ḥudūth al-ʿālam
This concerns whether the world has a beginning and whether an eternal being can produce a temporal event.
This is a classic kalam question.
3
Understand the second issue: Kharq al-ʿādāt
This is about divine intervention: can God create effects without their usual causes or create new causes? This is related to miracles.
4
Reflect on the connection
How do these two issues relate to the earlier three divisions? They provide the theoretical foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب، أن هذا استمداد مسألة قدم العالم، وأن المشيئة قديمة، من فليكن العالم قديماً، وقد أبطلنا ذلك وبينا أنه لا يبعد في العقل، وضع ثلاثة أقسام، وهى: أن يكون الله تعالى موجوداً، ولا عالم. ثم يخلق العالم، على النظام المشاهد ثم يستأنف نظاماً ثانياً، وهو الموعود في الجنة. ثم يعدم الكل، حتى لا يبقى إلا الله تعالى، وهو ممكن، لولا أن الشرع قد ورد، بأن الثواب والعقاب، والجنة والنار، لا آخر لها وهذه المسألة كيفما دارت تنبنى على مسألتين: إحداهما: حدوث العالم، وجواز حصول حادث من قديم. والثانية: خرق العادات، بخلق المسببات، دون الأسباب، أو إحداث أسباب، على منهج آخر غير معتاد، وقد فرغنا من المسألتين جميعاً، والله أعلم.
And the answer: that this is deriving from the issue of the eternity of the world, and that the will is eternal, so let the world be eternal. And we have refuted that and shown that it is not rationally remote to posit three divisions, which are: that Allah exists and no world (exists). Then He creates the world in the observed order. Then He initiates a second order, which is promised in Paradise. Then He annihilates everything, such that nothing remains except Allah. And that is possible, were it not that the divine law has come that reward and punishment, and Paradise and Hell have no end. And this issue, however it is considered, is based on two issues: one of them: the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) and the permissibility of an originated thing occurring from an eternal being. And the second: the breaking of habits (kharq al-ʿādāt) by creating effects without their causes, or creating causes on a different pattern than the usual. And we have finished both issues. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is an answer and conclusion to the previous argument. Ghazali first states that the philosophers' reasoning (that the eternal will implies a regular repetitive order) is merely a borrowing from the issue of the world's eternity (qidam al-ʿālam). They assume the will is eternal and therefore the world must be eternal. But Ghazali claims he has already refuted that assumption and shown that it is not rationally impossible (lā yubʿud fī al-ʿaql) to posit instead three divisions (aqsām) of the divine acts: 1) God exists alone without any world; 2) then He creates the world in the observed order; 3) then He initiates a second order, that is the promised order in Paradise; 4) then He annihilates everything so that only God remains. This fourth step is possible except that the revealed law (sharʿ) states that reward, punishment, Paradise, and Hell are everlasting, so annihilation does not apply to them. Ghazali then notes that the whole issue (whether the world can have a beginning or must be eternal) depends on two more fundamental issues: (a) the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) and the possibility of an originated thing coming from an eternal agent; (b) the breaking of natural habits (kharq al-ʿādāt) – i.e., whether God can create effects without their usual causes or create new causes on an unconventional pattern. Ghazali declares that he has already fully treated both issues earlier in the book. This passage thus serves as a summary and transition, affirming the possibility of a non-eternal world and divine intervention, against the philosophers' necessitarian view.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
خرق العادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the second underlying issue that the whole discussion depends on?
A) The eternity of the will.
B) The breaking of habits by creating effects without causes.
C) The three divisions of divine acts.
D) The annihilation of the world.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali identifies the two pillars of the debate: whether the world has a beginning and whether God can suspend natural causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the world had a beginning, and that you are accountable to a Creator who brought it into existence? How does that belief affect your daily decisions and your sense of purpose?
?
If God can break His normal patterns at any moment—suspending cause and effect—does that terrify you or give you hope? Why? And how does that shape your trust in His power over your life?
?
When you face an impossible situation, do you pray expecting God to break His habits, or do you assume the causal chain is fixed? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on God versus your reliance on the world's order?

Pre-Eternity by Intentional Will: Al-Khayyali's Argument
Al-Khayyali's argument for pre-eternal voluntary creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Khayyali's argument that a pre-eternal act can proceed from divine intention without temporal precedence.

Core Idea
Intention can be ontologically prior but temporally concurrent with existence.

In this passage, al-Khayyali challenges the standard kalam position that every voluntary act must be temporal. He proposes that the divine intention can be pre-eternal and yet the act of bringing into existence can be concurrent with it in time, allowing the world to be pre-eternal without compromising God's freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compressed style.
2
Identify al-Taftazani's claim
Al-Taftazani asserts that any act proceeding by intention and choice is necessarily temporal. Find this in the passage.
This is the standard position.
3
Understand al-Khayyali's objection
Al-Khayyali objects by distinguishing between precedence by essence and by time. The intention can be prior in essence but concurrent in time.
Precedence by essence does not imply temporal priority.
4
Follow the analogy
The analogy: just as the act of bringing into existence (ijad) is prior to existence (wujud) by essence not time, so the intention (qasd) is prior to ijad by essence not time.
If the intention is pre-eternal, then existence can be pre-eternal.
5
Conclude the argument
If the intention is pre-eternal (no impossibility), then existence (the world) is pre-eternal. This is al-Khayyali's conclusion.
This preserves both will and pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل إن بعض العلماء الذين يعتبرون من أركان نهضة علم الكلام ، قد جوز القول بقدم العالم . ير فيه خطراً على العقيدة، ذلك هو « المولى الخيالى »، و « عبد الحكيم السيالكوتى »، إذ يقرر سعد الدين التفتازانى » شارح « العقائد النفسية ] أن القدم ينا فى العدم، لأن القديم إن كان واجباً لذاته ، فظاهر ، وإلا لزم استناده إليه بطريق الإيجاب ، إذ الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار ، يكون حادثاً بالضرورة . . . إلخ ] فلا يرضى ( الخيالي » عن قول « السعد »: الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار ، يكون حادثاً ] ويجوز أن يكون الشيء صادراً بالقصد والاختيار ، ومع ذلك يكون قديماً . وهذا نصه : ، واعترض عليه ، بجواز أن يكون تقدم القصد الكامل ، على الإيجاد ، كتقدم الإيجاد على الوجود ، فى أنه بحسب الذات لا الزمان فيكون مقارناً للوجود زماناً ] . ومتى جاز مقارنة القصد للإيجاد زماناً ، والايجاد مقارن للوجود زماناً أيضاً ، كان القصد مقارناً للوجود زماناً · فمتى كان القصد قديماً - وهذا ما لا استحالة فيه – كان الوجود قديماً ويعلق عبد الحكيم » على وجهة نظر ( الخيالي ( فيصورها تصويراً واضحاً ولا يعقب عليها بنقد ، مما يدل على رضاه عنها . أن وما دام خوف المتكلمين من القول بعدم العالم، راجعاً إلى ما يؤدى إليه ، من لزوم يكون الباري فاعلا بالإيجاب، وقد كان هذا هو المحور الذي يدور عليه خلاف الغزالي للفلاسفة ، · وم ما دام يمكن تفادى هذا اللازم ، فلا ضير إذن فى القول بقدم العالم ، إن صح دليله وشبيه بموقف «الخيالي» من هذه المسألة ، موقف «توماس الإكويني ١٢٢٥ – ١٢٧٤ » حيث يقول : الإرادة الحرة ، لا يمكن الفحص عنها بالنظر الصرف ، فقد يكون الله خلق العالم منذ القدم ، وقد يكون خلقه فى الزمان ، ولا يمكن إثبات أحد الطرفين بالبرهان ] .
Rather, some scholars who are considered pillars of the revival of the science of kalam allowed the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, seeing no danger in it for creed. They are al-Mawla al-Khayyali and Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti, as Sad al-Din al-Taftazani, commentator on al-Aqaid al-Nasafiyya, decides that pre-eternity contradicts non-existence, because if the eternal is necessary by itself, it is obvious, otherwise it would require being dependent on it by way of necessitation, since that which proceeds from a thing by intention and choice is necessarily temporal... etc. So al-Khayyali is not satisfied with al-Sa'd's statement: 'that which proceeds from a thing by intention and choice is temporal' and he deems it permissible that a thing proceed by intention and choice and yet be pre-eternal. And this is his text: 'And it is objected to, by the permissibility that the precedence of the complete intention over the act of bringing into existence is like the precedence of the act of bringing into existence over existence, in that it is according to essence not time, so it is temporally concurrent with existence.' And whenever it is permissible for the intention to be temporally concurrent with the act of bringing into existence, and the act of bringing into existence is also temporally concurrent with existence, then the intention is temporally concurrent with existence. And so when the intention is pre-eternal - and there is no impossibility in this - then existence is pre-eternal. And Abd al-Hakim comments on al-Khayyali's viewpoint, depicting it clearly and not criticizing it, indicating his approval of it. And as long as the mutakallimun's fear of the statement of the non-eternity of the world is due to what it leads to, of the necessity of the Creator being an agent by necessitation, and this was the axis around which Ghazali's disagreement with the philosophers revolved, and as long as this necessary consequence can be avoided, then there is no harm in stating the pre-eternity of the world if its proof is sound. And similar to al-Khayyali's position on this issue is the position of Thomas Aquinas (1225-1274) where he says: 'Free will cannot be investigated by pure reasoning; God may have created the world from eternity, and He may have created it in time, and neither of the two can be proven by demonstration.'
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses certain scholars who allowed the pre-eternity of the world. It begins by naming al-Khayyali and al-Siyalkuti as proponents. It quotes al-Taftazani's argument that a voluntary act must be temporal. Al-Khayyali objects that an act can be pre-eternal if the intention precedes the act qualitatively, not temporally, similar to how the act of bringing into existence precedes the existence in essence but not time. He argues that if the intention is pre-eternal, the existence can be pre-eternal. Al-Siyalkuti supports this view. The passage then compares this to Thomas Aquinas's view that free will cannot be investigated by pure reason, and that the world could be pre-eternal or created in time, neither being provable.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
قصد
إيجاد
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any object of will must be temporally posterior to the act of will.
Correction: It can be ontologically posterior but temporally concurrent, as argued by al-Khayyali.

Checkpoint
According to al-Khayyali, what is the relationship between the intention (qasd) and the act of bringing into existence (ijad)?
A) The intention must temporally precede the act
B) The intention is ontologically prior but temporally concurrent
C) The intention is identical to the act
D) The intention is unnecessary for a pre-eternal act

Key Takeaway
Al-Khayyali argues that a pre-eternal intention can result in a pre-eternal existence if the precedence is by essence, not time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just encountered a distinction between essential and temporal precedence. How does this challenge your everyday assumption that ‘cause’ must mean ‘before in time’? In what way might your understanding of God’s actions be reshaped if you take this distinction seriously?
?
This argument allows that God’s intention and the world’s existence could be co-eternal. Does this possibility unsettle you? Why? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to a purely temporal understanding of creation?
?
If intention can be ontologically prior without being temporally prior, then God’s will is not constrained by time. How does this affect your view of God’s relationship with the world? Are you willing to let go of a ‘before and after’ picture of creation?

Theological Implications: Ghazali's Concern and Aquinas's Agnosticism
Al-Siyalkuti's approval and comparison with Aquinas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Khayyali's argument avoids the problem of divine necessitation and how it parallels Thomas Aquinas's view on the limits of reason regarding the world's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If pre-eternity does not imply necessitation, it becomes theologically permissible.

The second part of the passage connects al-Khayyali's argument to the broader theological debate. The mutakallimun's fear of pre-eternity stemmed from its perceived implication that God acts by necessitation. But if that implication can be avoided, the door is open. The passage also draws a parallel with Thomas Aquinas, who held that reason cannot prove whether the world is pre-eternal or created in time, leaving it a matter of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the flow of ideas.
This part focuses on the consequences and comparisons.
2
Identify Ghazali's objection
The mutakallimun feared that pre-eternity would mean God acts by necessitation, which was Ghazali's critique of the philosophers.
Necessitation (ijab) conflicts with free will.
3
Understand how the objection is avoided
Al-Khayyali's argument shows that pre-eternity can be by will, not necessitation, thus avoiding the feared consequence.
This makes pre-eternity theologically permissible.
4
Examine the parallel with Aquinas
Thomas Aquinas held that reason alone cannot determine if the world is pre-eternal or created in time; both are possible.
This aligns with al-Khayyali's position that pre-eternity is not rationally impossible.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the passage demonstrates that theological positions can be reevaluated when new arguments remove old fears.
Both reason and revelation have their roles.

Ghazali's Answer to the Time-Before-Creation Dilemma
Objection-and-answer on the nature of time before creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali's claim that time is created resolves the philosophical dilemma about the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Time itself is created, so the dilemma of 'before creation' is invalid.

The philosophers argue that if the world was created, the time before it must be either finite or infinite, both problematic. Ghazali's response is elegant: time is not an eternal backdrop but a created thing. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the objection and the concise answer.
Notice how Ghazali presents both horns of the dilemma before giving his solution.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The philosophers claim that if the duration before creation was finite, God would have a beginning; if infinite, it could not have ended. Grasp why each is problematic for the idea of creation.
3
Analyze Ghazali's answer
Ghazali states that duration and time are created. This cuts the dilemma: if time is created with the world, there is no 'before' time. Ask yourself how this answers both horns.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view aligns with the Ash'arī position that time is an accident (of motion) and thus dependent on created things.
This also hints at the idea that time will return (possibly referring to the afterlife).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض الفلاسفة على القول بحدوث العالم : بأن المدة التي سبقت خلق العالم كانت متناهية ؟ أم غير متناهية ؟ إن قلتم متناهية ، كان لوجود الله أول . وإن قلتم غير متناهية ، فكيف انقضى ما لا نهاية له ؟ الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض ، أن المدة والزمان مخلوقان ، وسيعود .
The philosophers' objection to the statement that the world is created: that the duration that preceded the creation of the world was finite? Or infinite? If you say finite, then God's existence would have a beginning. And if you say infinite, then how does that which has no end come to an end? The answer to this objection: that duration and time are created, and will return.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a standard kalām objection-and-answer regarding the eternity of the world. The philosophers pose a dilemma: either the time before creation was finite or infinite. If finite, then God's existence would have a beginning (since He existed before time began, implying a first moment of existence). If infinite, then an infinite duration would have passed, which is impossible. Ghazali's answer is decisive: time itself is a created entity, not an independent container. Since God created time along with the world, the dilemma dissolves. The objection mistakenly assumes time is eternal and independent. Ghazali also hints that time will return (referring to the afterlife?). This answer is characteristic of the Ash'arī position that time is an accident (of motion) and thus created.

Key Terms
المدة (duration)
الزمان (time)
مخلوقان (created)
متناهية (finite)
غير متناهية (infinite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an eternal, independent container that existed before creation.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that time and duration are created entities, not an eternal backdrop.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's answer to the philosophers' dilemma about the time before creation?
A) Time is eternal and uncreated.
B) Time and duration are created and will return.
C) The world is eternal.
D) God existed before time in a temporal sense.

Key Takeaway
Time is a created entity, not an eternal container; thus, the question of what happened before time is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself anxious about God's 'timing' in your life, do you realize that you are imposing a created framework of time onto the Eternal? How can this lesson transform your waiting into trust? Will you actively remind yourself that Allah is beyond time when you feel He is 'late'?
?
Reflect on a moment when you believed that something 'had to happen by a certain time' or that God was constrained by deadlines. Where did that assumption come from? Does Ghazali's argument expose your belief as rooted in a flawed view of time? What specific step will you take this week to surrender your timeline to Allah?
?
If time is created and will return (end), then your entire life is bounded by time. Does this make you feel trapped or liberated? How does knowing that time is a creature—not a cage—change the urgency of your worship? Commit to one action today that recognizes your time as a gift from the Creator.

The Philosophers' Second Proof for the Eternity of the World
Philosophers argue from God's essential priority to the eternity of both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof for the eternity of the world based on the nature of God's priority over creation.

Core Idea
If God's priority is essential, the world must share His eternal status.

The philosophers' second proof for the eternity of the world relies on a simple but powerful logical inference about the nature of God's priority over the world. By examining what it means for God to be 'prior', they conclude that the world must be eternal. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the key terms 'متقدم' (prior), 'بالذات' (in essence), 'بالزمان' (in time).
2
Identify the key premise
The agreed premise: God is prior to the world. The debate is about the nature of this priority.
3
Understand the two options
If the priority is essential (not temporal), then both God and the world must share the same temporal status: both eternal or both temporal.
This is a logical consequence of causation sharing temporal modality.
4
Why the temporal option is impossible
The philosophers argue that the world cannot be temporal because that would lead to an infinite regress or contradiction. Hence it must be eternal.
The text says 'كونهما حادثين محال' (their being both temporal is impossible).
5
Conclusion: both are eternal
Therefore, since the world is not temporal, it must be eternal, just like God.
The conclusion is 'فثبت أن الله والعالم قديمان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثان للفلاسفة في إثبات قدم العالم قال الفلاسفة : إن الله متقدم على العالم بالاتفاق ؛ فإن أريد أنه متقدم عليه بالذات لا بالزمان، لزم أن يكون الله والعالم إما قديمين أو حادثين ، وكونهما حادثين محال ، فثبت أن الله والعالم قديمان ..
A second proof for the philosophers in establishing the eternity of the world. The philosophers said: God is prior to the world by agreement; if it is intended that He is prior to it in essence, not in time, then it necessarily follows that God and the world are either both eternal or both temporal. And their being both temporal is impossible, so it is established that God and the world are both eternal.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the world's eternity. They argue from the universally accepted premise that God is prior to the world. If this priority is essential (i.e., God is the cause, not temporally prior), then both God and the world must share the same temporal status: both eternal or both temporal. Since the world cannot be temporal (because that would require a temporal cause, leading to an infinite regress), it follows that the world is eternal like God. This is a classical argument for coeternity.

Key Terms
متقدم
بالذات
بالزمان
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God and the world are identical in essence or equal.
Correction: The argument is about temporal status, not essence. Both are eternal, but God is the cause and the world is the effect; they are not identical.

Checkpoint
According to this proof, why must the world be eternal?
A) Because the world exists forever by itself.
B) Because if God is prior in essence, the world cannot be temporal without contradiction.
C) Because the philosophers proved it through revelation.
D) Because the world is uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that if God is essentially prior to the world, then the world must be eternal, coeternal with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is essentially prior to all things, what does that mean for your daily reliance on created causes? Do you treat secondary causes as if they were independent, or do you see them as constantly dependent on God's essential priority? This week, how can you consciously acknowledge God's causal priority in a specific situation where you tend to rely on your own ability?
?
The philosophers argue that the world must be coeternal with God to avoid a temporal effect from an eternal cause. But in your own life, do you ever feel that some effects (like your past sins) are too permanent to be changed by an eternal cause? How does this argument challenge or encourage you to trust that God's eternal priority can bring about a new beginning in your life, even when your past feels fixed?
?
Consider something about yourself that you believe is 'essential'—a characteristic you think defines you. The philosophers' proof shows that essential priority implies a necessary relationship. What would it mean for your identity if you saw yourself as essentially dependent on God? Are there areas where you act as if you are independent and self-existing? What specific change would show that you truly believe God is your essential priority?

The World's Contingency Implies Size Variation and Creation Ex Nihilo
Ghazali argues from world's size variability to contingency and creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the world's capability of being larger or smaller to argue for its contingency and the possibility of creation from nothing.

Core Idea
The world's changeability proves it is not necessary, pointing to creation ex nihilo.

Ghazali confronts the philosophers' claim that the world cannot be larger or smaller. He argues that if it were not capable of such change, it would be necessary, but the world is contingent. This change implies the annihilation or creation of matter, supporting the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo. In this passage, he also critiques the notion of 'possible before existence' as meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the overall argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the conditional: if the world cannot be larger/smaller, then it is necessary. Then the negation: the world is not necessary. Therefore, the conclusion: it can be larger/smaller.
3
Understand the counter to philosophers
Ghazali considers the philosophical objection that size change is impossible. He replies that possibility before existence is meaningless; only actual existence matters.
This refutes the idea that the world could have been created earlier.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports creation ex nihilo. The change requires annihilation or creation of matter, showing matter is not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرى الغزالى أن العالم إذا لم يكن قابلا لأن يكون أكبر مما هو عليه أو أصغر ، فإنه يكون واجباً . والعالم ليس بواجب فإذن هو قابل لأن يكون أكبر أو أصغر ؛ وذلك يكون بإعدام جزء من المادة ، أو خلق جزء منها وهذا يفيد إمكان خلق المادة من لا شيء ، وصيرورتها إلى ما أشرنا إليه فى مقدمة الطبعة الثالثة، فكيف ساغ لا شيء وهو لبعض الباحثين أن يدعوا بداهة استحالة ذلك ؟ يقول الغزالى : إذا ساغ للفلاسفة أن يقولوا : إن كون العالم أكبر مما هو عليه أصغر ، مستحيل ، عارضناهم بقولنا : إن وجود العالم قبل وجوده لم يكن ممكناً ، وإنما الممكن وجوده في الوقت الذي وجد فيه ويعود الغزالي فيقرر أن ما يدعيه الفلاسفة من إمكان خلق العالم قبل خلقه بمدة، ثم بمدة أطول وهكذا ، كلام لا معنى له عند التحقيق
Al-Ghazali holds that if the world were not capable of being larger than it is or smaller, then it would be necessary. And the world is not necessary; therefore it is capable of being larger or smaller. And that would be by annihilating part of matter or creating part of it. And this indicates the possibility of creating matter from nothing, and its transformation to what we pointed to in the introduction to the third edition. So how is it permissible for some researchers to claim the obvious impossibility of that? Al-Ghazali says: 'If it is permissible for philosophers to say that it is impossible for the world to be larger or smaller, we oppose them by saying that the existence of the world before its existence was not possible; rather, what is possible is its existence at the time it came to exist.' And al-Ghazali returns to affirm that what the philosophers claim about the possibility of creating the world a period before its creation, then a longer period, and so on, is meaningless upon investigation.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the world's contingency. Ghazali reasons: if the world could not be larger or smaller, it would be necessary (i.e., it would have to be exactly as it is). But the world is not necessary (it is contingent), so it must be able to be larger or smaller. This change implies either annihilation or creation of matter, which shows the possibility of creation ex nihilo. Ghazali then counters a philosophical claim that the world cannot be larger or smaller: he says that the world's existence before it existed was not possible, only at the time it actually came to exist. So the idea of creating it earlier is meaningless.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib, necessary)
ممكن (mumkin, possible)
مادة (mādda, matter)
لا شيء (lā shay', nothing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That contingency implies possibility of earlier creation.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'possible before existence' is meaningless; only actual time matters.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what follows if the world were not capable of being larger or smaller?
A) It would be impossible
B) It would be necessary
C) It would be eternal
D) It would be infinite

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the world's capacity for size change to prove its contingency and the possibility of creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's very size shows its dependence on God, why do you still live as though your existence is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly believed that every moment you exist is a gift from nothing?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that the possibility of creation before actual existence is meaningless. Where in your life are you clinging to possibilities that never materialized, delaying your submission to God's timing?
?
The world could have been larger or smaller—it is not necessary. Does your trust in God reflect that He alone is the Necessary? Or do you treat worldly things as if they must be a certain way, and how might you realign your heart today?

The Argument from Possible Size for Contingency and Creation Ex Nihilo
Ghazali presents the argument that the world's possible change in size proves contingency and refutes impossibility of creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the world's potential to change size to argue for its contingency and the possibility of creation from nothing.

Core Idea
If the world can change size, it is contingent and matter can be created from nothing.

This passage presents a logical argument central to Ghazali's critique of the philosophers. He begins with a conditional: if something were necessary, it would be immutable in size. Since the world can be larger or smaller, it is contingent, implying that matter can be created or annihilated. This leads to the possibility of creation ex nihilo. Ghazali then turns the philosophers' own reasoning against them, showing that their claim of impossibility is unfounded.

Guided Path

The conditional is key: necessity implies immutability in size.
2

Notice that the world is not necessary, so it must be capable of being larger or smaller. This establishes the world's contingency.
Contingency means the world depends on something else for its existence and properties.
3
Draw the conclusion
The possibility of change in size involves annihilation or creation of matter, which implies creation ex nihilo. Reflect on how Ghazali uses this to refute the philosophers' claim of impossibility.
The conclusion directly challenges the philosophers' notion that creation from nothing is impossible.

Eternal Possibility Does Not Imply Eternal Existence
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' third proof for the world's eternity by denying that eternal possibility entails eternal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali’s refutation of the philosophers’ third argument for the eternity of the world, specifically why eternal possibility does not necessitate eternal existence.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not force eternal existence.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a key philosophical argument that claims the world is eternal because its possibility is eternal. He counters by pointing out that possibility, no matter how eternal, does not compel actual existence. This is a crucial move in the debate over the world's origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the structure: the philosophers' claim, Ghazali's response, and the analogy.
The passage is short but dense. Read carefully.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice Ghazali's comparison: the possibility of an infinite increase in the size of the world does not entail the existence of an infinite number of beings. Understand how this analogy supports his point.
The word 'ملاء' likely refers to angels or a host of beings.
3
Understand the logical point
Reflect on why possibility does not entail actuality. Possibility is a modal concept; actual existence requires additional conditions. Eternal possibility of the world does not make the world eternal.
This refutes the philosophers' inference from possibility to necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث للفلاسفة على قدم العالم بحث في أزلية الإمكان * * ويرى الغزالى أن الإمكان الأزلي لا يستلزم الوجود الأزلى ، مثلما لم يستلزم إمكان زيادة لا نهاية لها في حجم العالم ، إمكان وجود ملاء لا نهاية له
A third proof of the philosophers for the eternity of the world: a discussion on the eternity of possibility. And al-Ghazali holds that eternal possibility does not entail eternal existence, just as the possibility of an infinite increase in the size of the world does not entail the possibility of the existence of an infinite [host/angels].
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage introduces the third philosophical proof for the world's eternity, which argues from the eternity of possibility. Ghazali's response is presented: eternal possibility does not imply eternal actual existence. He uses an analogy: the possibility of an infinite increase in the world's size does not prove that an infinite number of beings (likely angels) actually exist. The possibility of something does not force its existence.

Key Terms
إمكان أزلي
الوجود الأزلي
لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal possibility necessitates eternal existence.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that possibility can be eternal; he denies that it necessitates actual existence. Possibility and actuality are distinct categories.

Checkpoint

A) The possibility of a different size world does not imply actual change.
B) The possibility of infinite increase in size does not imply an actual infinite multitude.
C) The possibility of creation before time does not imply eternal matter.
D) The possibility of the world being larger does not make it necessary.

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility does not force anything into existence; possibility and actuality are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ghazali's First Argument: Impossibility Would Also Need a Subject
Ghazali argues that if possibility requires a subject, impossibility would too.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if possibility required a subject, impossibility would also require one, undermining the philosophers' claim.

Core Idea
If possibility needs a subject, so does impossibility.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' assumption that possibility must inhere in an eternal subject. He uses a parallel argument: if that were true, impossibility would also need a subject, which is absurd. This lesson examines that first counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp Ghazali's point. Notice how he draws a parallel between possibility and impossibility.
The argument is simple but powerful.
2
Identify the logical structure
Ghazali uses a conditional: if possibility requires a subject, then the same applies to impossibility. Consider why this is problematic for the philosophers.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this argument means for the philosophers' position. If impossibility would need a subject, it shows that their reasoning is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويضيف الغزالي : أنه لو استدعى الإمكان شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه و إن البياض والسواد ممكنان قبل وجودهما ، فإن كان الإمكان وصفاً الذي يطرآن عليه ، كان إمكاناً للجسم لا لهما ، للجسم والمفروض أنهما الممكنان و إن نفوس الآدميين عند ابن سينا حادة ومجردة عن المادة ، فبماذا قام إمكانها قبل وجودها ؟.
And al-Ghazali adds: If possibility required an existent thing to be attributed to it, then impossibility would also require an existent thing to be attributed to it. And white and black are possible before their existence; if possibility is an attribute that inheres in the subject they occur upon, it would be a possibility of the body, not of them, for the body while it is assumed that they are the possibles. And the human souls according to Ibn Sina are created and immaterial, so in what did their possibility subsist before their existence?
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents three additional arguments by Ghazali against the philosophers' claim that possibility requires an eternal subject. First, if possibility needed a subject, then impossibility (الامتناع) would also need a subject, which is absurd. Second, consider accidents like whiteness and blackness: before they exist, they are possible. If possibility were an attribute of a subject, it would belong to the body in which they inhere, not to the accidents themselves, yet we say the accidents are possible. Third, Ibn Sina holds that human souls are created (حادثة) and immaterial (مجردة عن المادة). If possibility required a material subject, what would support the possibility of souls before their existence? This challenges the necessity of an eternal material subject.

Key Terms
الامتناع (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only possibility requires a subject.
Correction: If the principle were true, it would apply to both possibility and impossibility, leading to an absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence Ghazali draws from the philosophers' claim that possibility needs a subject?
A) Impossibility also needs a subject.
B) Possibility is impossible.
C) Impossibility does not need a subject.
D) The subject must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first argument shows that the philosophers' principle leads to the absurd conclusion that impossibility would need a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or His attributes without critically examining the logical foundation of that belief? What assumption are you holding onto that you have not tested against clear reasoning like Ghazali does here?
?
This argument shows that a seemingly reasonable principle can lead to an absurdity. Is there any area of your faith where you cling to an idea because it 'feels' true, even when it doesn't hold up to scrutiny? Will you commit to examining it today?
?
If the philosophers' flawed reasoning could have misled them about the existence of a creator, could your own unexamined assumptions be pulling you away from a correct understanding of Allah? What step will you take this week to strengthen your convictions?

Ghazali's Second Argument: Accidents' Possibility Belongs to Bodies
Ghazali argues that accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if possibility were an attribute of a subject, then accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents themselves.

Core Idea
Accidents' possibility actually belongs to the body, not the accident.

Ghazali uses the example of whiteness and blackness to show a contradiction in the philosophers' view. If possibility is an attribute that inheres in a subject, then before accidents exist, their possibility would belong to the body they occur on, not to the accidents themselves. But we say the accidents are possible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path

This shows a contradiction.
3

First, if possibility needed a subject, then impossibility (الامتناع) would also need a subject, which is absurd. Second, consider accidents like whiteness and blackness: before they exist, they are possible. If possibility were an attribute of a subject, it would belong to the body in which they inhere, not to the accidents themselves, yet we say the accidents are possible. Third, Ibn Sina holds that human souls are created (حادثة) and immaterial (مجردة عن المادة). If possibility required a material subject, what would support the possibility of souls before their existence? This challenges the necessity of an eternal material subject.

Key Terms
العرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents' possibility is independent of the body.
Correction: If possibility required a subject, it would be the body's possibility, not the accident's, contradicting our language.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, if possibility is an attribute of the subject, whose possibility is it?
A) The accident's
B) The body's
C) The subject's
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second argument shows that accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents, contradicting the philosophers' assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'It is possible that Allah will forgive me,' where does that possibility reside—in your expectation or in Allah’s will? Ghazali shows that philosophical categories can mislead us about reality. Are there places in your faith where you have accepted a human system of thought instead of the simple truth of the Quran and Sunnah? What will you do to untangle that?
?
Ghazali exposed a flaw in the philosophers' reasoning using the everyday example of whiteness and blackness. Think of one area of your life—your worship, your relationships, your understanding of God—where you have overcomplicated things. Are you willing to set aside that complexity and return to the simple, clear guidance of revelation? If so, what is your first step today?

Ghazali's Third Argument: Immaterial Souls' Possibility
Ghazali questions how immaterial souls' possibility could subsist without a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that immaterial souls, according to Ibn Sina, would have no subject for their possibility, challenging the need for an eternal material subject.

Core Idea
Immaterial souls have no material subject for their possibility.

Ghazali targets the philosophers' own view on human souls: Ibn Sina holds that souls are created and immaterial. If possibility requires a subject, what subject would support the possibility of souls before they exist? This lesson examines that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that according to Ibn Sina, souls are immaterial and created (حادثة).
The word 'حادة' is likely a misspelling of 'حادثة' (created).
2
Identify the contradiction
If souls are immaterial, they cannot have a material subject. So the philosophers' claim that possibility needs a subject fails for souls.
3
Reflect on the outcome
This argument uses the philosophers' own doctrines against them. If they accept immaterial souls, they must accept that possibility can exist without a material subject.
